class="mergedrow"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Tree</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Ginkgo_biloba" title="Ginkgo biloba">Ginkgo</a></td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Bird</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Black-headed_gull" title="Black-headed gull">Black-headed gull</a></td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Website</th><td class="infobox-data"><span class="url"><a class="external text" href="http://www.metro.tokyo.lg.jp/" <p><b>Tokyo</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">東京</span>, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Tōkyō</i></span>, <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="ja-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Japanese" title="Help:IPA/Japanese">[toːkʲoː]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ja-Tokyo.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cf/Ja-Tokyo.ogg" title="Ja-Tokyo.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>), officially the <b>Tokyo Metropolis</b> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">東京都</span></span>, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Tōkyō-to</i></span>), is the capital and <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Japan" title="List of cities in Japan">largest city</a> of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Formerly known as <b>Edo</b>, its metropolitan area (spanning 13,452 square kilometres or 5,194 square miles) is the most populous in the world, with an estimated 37.468 million residents as of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN-World-Cities-2018_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN-World-Cities-2018-9">[9]</a></sup> the city proper has a population of 13.99 million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Located at the head of <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Bay" title="Tokyo Bay">Tokyo Bay</a>, the prefecture forms part of the <a href="/wiki/Kant%C5%8D_region" title="Kantō region">Kantō region</a> on the central coast of <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshu</a>, Japan's largest island. Tokyo serves as <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Japan" title="Economy of Japan">Japan's economic centre</a> and is the seat of both the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Japan" title="Government of Japan">Japanese government</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">Emperor of Japan</a>. 
</p><p>Originally a fishing village named <a href="/wiki/Edo" title="Edo">Edo</a>, the city became a prominent political centre in 1603, when it became the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa shogunate</a>. By the mid-18th century, Edo was one of the most populous cities in the world with a population of over one million people. Following the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a> of 1868, the imperial capital in <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a> was moved to Edo, which was renamed "Tokyo" (<abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;"> </span>'<a href="/wiki/Capital_of_Japan" title="Capital of Japan">Eastern Capital</a>'). Tokyo was devastated by the <a href="/wiki/1923_Great_Kant%C5%8D_earthquake" title="1923 Great Kantō earthquake">1923 Great Kantō earthquake</a>, and again by <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Tokyo" title="Bombing of Tokyo">Allied bombing raids during World War II</a>. Beginning in the 1950s, the city underwent rapid reconstruction and expansion efforts, going on to lead the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_economic_miracle" title="Japanese economic miracle">Japanese economic miracle</a>. Since 1943, the <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Government" title="Tokyo Metropolitan Government">Tokyo Metropolitan Government</a> has administered the prefecture's <a href="/wiki/Special_wards_of_Tokyo" title="Special wards of Tokyo">23 special wards</a> (formerly <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_City" title="Tokyo City">Tokyo City</a>), various commuter towns and suburbs in its western area, and two outlying island chains known as the <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Islands" title="Tokyo Islands">Tokyo Islands</a>.
</p><p>Tokyo is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_by_GDP" title="List of cities by GDP">largest urban economy worldwide by gross domestic product</a>, and is categorized as an <a href="/wiki/Global_city" title="Global city">Alpha+ city</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Globalization_and_World_Cities_Research_Network" title="Globalization and World Cities Research Network">Globalization and World Cities Research Network</a>. It is also Japan's leading centre of business and finance as part of an industrial region that includes the cities of <a href="/wiki/Yokohama" title="Yokohama">Yokohama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kawasaki,_Kanagawa" title="Kawasaki, Kanagawa">Kawasaki</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chiba_(city)" title="Chiba (city)">Chiba</a>. As of 2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Tokyo is home to 37 companies of the <a href="/wiki/Fortune_Global_500#Fortune_Global_500_of_2021" title="Fortune Global 500"><i>Fortune</i> Global 500</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In 2020, the city ranked fourth on the <a href="/wiki/Global_Financial_Centres_Index" title="Global Financial Centres Index">Global Financial Centres Index</a>, behind only <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GFCI2_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-GFCI2-12">[12]</a></sup> Tokyo is home to the world's tallest tower, the <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Skytree" title="Tokyo Skytree">Tokyo Skytree</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skytree_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-skytree-13">[13]</a></sup> and the world's largest underground floodwater diversion facility, the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Area_Outer_Underground_Discharge_Channel" title="Metropolitan Area Outer Underground Discharge Channel">Metropolitan Area Outer Underground Discharge Channel</a> (located in <a href="/wiki/Kasukabe,_Saitama" title="Kasukabe, Saitama">Kasukabe, Saitama</a>, a suburb of Tokyo).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maoudc_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-maoudc-14">[14]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Metro_Ginza_Line" title="Tokyo Metro Ginza Line">Tokyo Metro Ginza Line</a>, opened in 1927, is the oldest underground metro line in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90th-2017_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-90th-2017-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The city has hosted multiple international events, including the <a href="/wiki/1964_Summer_Olympics" title="1964 Summer Olympics">1964 Summer Olympics</a> and <a href="/wiki/1964_Summer_Paralympics" title="1964 Summer Paralympics">1964 Summer Paralympics</a>, the <a href="/wiki/2020_Summer_Olympics" title="2020 Summer Olympics">2020 Summer Olympics</a> and <a href="/wiki/2020_Summer_Paralympics" title="2020 Summer Paralympics">2020 Summer Paralympics</a> (postponed; held in 2021), and three summits of the <a href="/wiki/G7" title="G7">G7</a> (in 1979, 1986, and 1993). Tokyo is an international centre leading <a href="/wiki/Research_and_development_in_Japan" title="Research and development in Japan">research and development in Japan</a> and is likewise represented by <a href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_Tokyo" title="List of universities in Tokyo">several major universities</a>, most notably including the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tokyo" title="University of Tokyo">University of Tokyo</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Station" title="Tokyo Station">Tokyo Station</a> is the central hub for Japan's high-speed railway network, the <a href="/wiki/Shinkansen" title="Shinkansen">Shinkansen</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Shinjuku_Station" title="Shinjuku Station">Shinjuku Station</a> in Tokyo is also the world's busiest train station. Notable special wards of Tokyo include: <a href="/wiki/Chiyoda,_Tokyo" title="Chiyoda, Tokyo">Chiyoda</a>, the site of the <a href="/wiki/National_Diet_Building" title="National Diet Building">National Diet Building</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Imperial_Palace" title="Tokyo Imperial Palace">Tokyo Imperial Palace</a>; <a href="/wiki/Shinjuku" title="Shinjuku">Shinjuku</a>, the city's administrative centre; and <a href="/wiki/Shibuya" title="Shibuya">Shibuya</a>, a commercial, cultural, and business hub.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-style: normal">Tōkyō</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Hepburn_romanization" title="Hepburn romanization">Revised Hepburn</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><span lang="ja-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Toukyou</span></span></td></tr></tbody></table></td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><span class="anchor" id="idEtymology"></span> Tokyo was originally known as <a href="/wiki/Edo" title="Edo">Edo</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%B1%9F%E6%88%B8" title="wikt:江戸">江戸</a></span></span>)</span>, a <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a> compound of <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%B1%9F" title="wikt:江">江</a> (<i>e</i>, "cove, inlet") and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%88%B8" title="wikt:戸">戸</a> (<i>to</i>, "entrance, gate, door").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naruto-san_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-naruto-san-16">[16]</a></sup> The name, which can be translated as "<a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuary</a>", is a reference to the original settlement's location at the meeting of the <a href="/wiki/Sumida_River" title="Sumida River">Sumida River</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Bay" title="Tokyo Bay">Tokyo Bay</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a> in 1868, the name of the city was changed to Tokyo<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9D%B1%E4%BA%AC" title="wikt:東京">東京</a></span></span>, from <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9D%B1" title="wikt:東">東</a></span></span></span> <i>tō</i> "east", and <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%BA%AC" title="wikt:京">京</a></span></span></span> <i>kyō</i> "capital")</span>, when it became the new imperial capital,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> in line with the East Asian tradition of including the word capital (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">京</span></span></span>) in the name of the capital city (for example, <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">京都</span></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Keijō</a> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">京城</span></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">北京</span></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">南京</span></span></span>), and <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Xijing_(disambiguation)" title="Xijing (disambiguation)">Xijing</a> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">西京</span></span></span>)).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naruto-san_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-naruto-san-16">[16]</a></sup> During the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meiji_period" title="Meiji period">Meiji period</a>, the city was sometimes called "Tōkei", an alternative pronunciation for the same characters representing "Tokyo", making it a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanji_homograph" title="Kanji homograph">kanji homograph</a>. Some surviving official English documents use the spelling "Tokei";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tōkei_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tōkei-18">[18]</a></sup> however, this pronunciation is now obsolete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Tokyo" title="History of Tokyo">History of Tokyo</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a chronological guide, see <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Tokyo" title="Timeline of Tokyo">Timeline of Tokyo</a>.</div>
<h3><span id="Pre-1869_.28Edo_period.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-1869_(Edo_period)">Pre-1869 (Edo period)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pre-1869 (Edo period)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Edo" title="Edo">Edo</a></div><p>
Tokyo was originally a village called Edo, in what was formerly part of the old <a href="/wiki/Musashi_Province" title="Musashi Province">Musashi Province</a>. Edo was first fortified by the <a href="/wiki/Edo_clan" title="Edo clan">Edo clan</a>, in the late twelfth century. In 1457, <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Cta_D%C5%8Dkan" title="Ōta Dōkan">Ōta Dōkan</a> built <a href="/wiki/Edo_Castle" title="Edo Castle">Edo Castle</a>. In 1590, <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Ieyasu" title="Tokugawa Ieyasu">Tokugawa Ieyasu</a> moved from <a href="/wiki/Mikawa_Province" title="Mikawa Province">Mikawa Province</a> (his lifelong base) to the <a href="/wiki/Kant%C5%8D_region" title="Kantō region">Kantō region</a>. When he became <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dgun" title="Shōgun">shōgun</a></i> in 1603, Edo became the center of his ruling. During the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a>, Edo grew into one of the largest cities in the world with a population topping one million by the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> But Edo was still the home of the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa shogunate</a> and not the <a href="/wiki/Capital_of_Japan" title="Capital of Japan">capital of Japan</a> (the Emperor himself lived in <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a> almost continuously from 794 to 1868).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> During the Edo era, the city enjoyed a prolonged period of peace known as the <i>Pax Tokugawa</i>, and in the presence of such peace, the shogunate adopted a stringent policy of seclusion, which helped to perpetuate the lack of any serious military threat to the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The absence of war-inflicted devastation allowed Edo to devote the majority of its resources to rebuilding in the wake of the consistent fires, earthquakes, and other devastating natural disasters that plagued the city. However, this prolonged period of seclusion came to an end with the arrival of American Commodore <a href="/wiki/Matthew_C._Perry" title="Matthew C. Perry">Matthew C. Perry</a> in 1853. Commodore Perry forced the opening of the ports of <a href="/wiki/Shimoda,_Shizuoka" title="Shimoda, Shizuoka">Shimoda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hakodate" title="Hakodate">Hakodate</a>, leading to an increase in the demand for new foreign goods and subsequently a severe rise in inflation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Social unrest mounted in the wake of these higher prices and culminated in widespread rebellions and demonstrations, especially in the form of the "smashing" of rice establishments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Meanwhile, supporters of the Emperor leveraged the disruption that these widespread rebellious demonstrations were causing to further consolidate power by overthrowing the last Tokugawa <i>shōgun</i>, <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Yoshinobu" title="Tokugawa Yoshinobu">Yoshinobu</a>, in 1867.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> After 265 years, the <i>Pax Tokugawa</i> came to an end. </p><div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:4008px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto;direction:rtl"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%27Kidai_Shoran%27,_Japanese_handscroll_c._1805.jpg" title="‪Kidai Shōran (熈代勝覧), 1805. It illustrates scenes from the Edo period taking place along the Nihonbashi in Tokyo.‬"><img alt="" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="9750" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/%27Kidai_Shoran%27%2C_Japanese_handscroll_c._1805.jpg/4000px-%27Kidai_Shoran%27%2C_Japanese_handscroll_c._1805.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/%27Kidai_Shoran%27%2C_Japanese_handscroll_c._1805.jpg/6000px-%27Kidai_Shoran%27%2C_Japanese_handscroll_c._1805.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/%27Kidai_Shoran%27%2C_Japanese_handscroll_c._1805.jpg/8000px-%27Kidai_Shoran%27%2C_Japanese_handscroll_c._1805.jpg 2x" width="4000"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%27Kidai_Shoran%27,_Japanese_handscroll_c._1805.jpg" title="File:'Kidai Shoran', Japanese handscroll c. 1805.jpg"> </a></div>Kidai Shōran<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">熈代勝覧</span></span>)</span>, 1805. It illustrates scenes from the Edo period taking place along the <a href="/wiki/Nihonbashi" title="Nihonbashi">Nihonbashi</a> in Tokyo.</div></div></div>

<h3><span id="1869.E2.80.931943"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1869–1943">1869–1943</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1869–1943">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_City" title="Tokyo City">Tokyo City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Prefecture_(1868%E2%80%931943)" title="Tokyo Prefecture (1868–1943)">Tokyo Prefecture (1868–1943)</a></div>
<p>Edo was renamed Tokyo (Eastern Capital) on 3 September 1868, as the new government was consolidating its power after the fall of the Edo shogunate. The young <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Meiji" title="Emperor Meiji">Emperor Meiji</a> visited once at the end of that year and eventually moved in in 1869. Tokyo was already the nation's political center,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and the emperor's residence made it a de facto imperial capital as well, with the former Edo Castle becoming the <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Imperial_Palace" title="Tokyo Imperial Palace">Imperial Palace</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_City" title="Tokyo City">city of Tokyo</a> was officially established on May 1, 1889.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Metro_Ginza_Line" title="Tokyo Metro Ginza Line">Tokyo Metro Ginza Line</a> portion between <a href="/wiki/Ueno_Station" title="Ueno Station">Ueno</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asakusa_Station_(Tokyo_Metro,_Toei,_Tobu)" title="Asakusa Station (Tokyo Metro, Toei, Tobu)">Asakusa</a> was the first subway line built in Japan and East Asia completed on December 30, 1927.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90th-2017_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-90th-2017-15">[15]</a></sup> Central Tokyo, like <a href="/wiki/Osaka" title="Osaka">Osaka</a>, has been designed since about 1900 to be centered on major railway stations in a high-density fashion, so suburban railways were built relatively cheaply at street level and with their own <a href="/wiki/Right-of-way_(transportation)" title="Right-of-way (transportation)">right-of-way</a>. Though <a href="/wiki/Shuto_Expressway" title="Shuto Expressway">expressways</a> have been built in Tokyo, the basic design has not changed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Tokyo went on to suffer two major catastrophes in the 20th century: the <a href="/wiki/1923_Great_Kant%C5%8D_earthquake" title="1923 Great Kantō earthquake">1923 Great Kantō earthquake</a>, which left 140,000 dead or missing; and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed" style="text-align: center;">
<li class="gallerycaption">Gallery</li>

</ul>
<h3><span id="1943.E2.80.931945"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1943–1945">1943–1945</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1943–1945">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Tokyo" title="Bombing of Tokyo">Bombing of Tokyo</a></div>
<p>In 1943, the city of Tokyo merged with the prefecture of Tokyo to form the "Metropolitan Prefecture" of Tokyo. Since then, the <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Government" title="Tokyo Metropolitan Government">Tokyo Metropolitan Government</a> served as both the prefecture government for Tokyo, as well as administering the <a href="/wiki/Special_wards_of_Tokyo" title="Special wards of Tokyo">special wards of Tokyo</a>, for what had previously been Tokyo City. <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> wreaked widespread destruction of most of the city due to the persistent <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> <a href="/wiki/Air_raids_on_Japan" title="Air raids on Japan">air raids on Japan</a> and the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incendiary_bombs" title="Incendiary bombs">incendiary bombs</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Tokyo" title="Bombing of Tokyo">bombing of Tokyo</a> in 1944 and 1945 is estimated to have killed between 75,000 and 200,000 civilians and left more than half of the city destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The deadliest night of the war came on March 9–10, 1945, the night of the American "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Meetinghouse" title="Operation Meetinghouse">Operation Meetinghouse</a>" raid;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> as nearly 700,000 incendiary bombs rained on the eastern half of the city, mainly in heavily residential wards. Two-fifths of the city were completely burned, more than 276,000 buildings were demolished, 100,000 civilians were killed, and 110,000 more were injured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Between 1940 and 1945, the population of Japan's capital city dwindled from 6,700,000 to less than 2,800,000, with the majority of those who lost their homes living in "ramshackle, makeshift huts".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed" style="text-align: center;">
<li class="gallerycaption">Gallery</li>

<h3><span id="1945.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1945–present">1945–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1945–present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the war, Tokyo became the base from which the United States under <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a> administered Japan for six years. Tokyo struggled to rebuild as <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Japan" title="Occupation of Japan">occupation authorities</a> stepped in and drastically cut back on Japanese government rebuilding programs, focusing instead on simply improving roads and transportation. Tokyo did not experience fast economic growth until the 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the occupation of Japan ended in 1952, Tokyo was completely rebuilt and was showcased to the world during the <a href="/wiki/1964_Summer_Olympics" title="1964 Summer Olympics">1964 Summer Olympics</a>. The 1970s and the 1980s brought new high-rise developments. In 1978, <a href="/wiki/Sunshine_60" title="Sunshine 60">Sunshine 60</a> – the tallest skyscraper in Asia until 1985, and in Japan until 1991<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skyscraperpage_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-skyscraperpage-34">[34]</a></sup> – and <a href="/wiki/Narita_International_Airport" title="Narita International Airport">Narita International Airport</a> were constructed, and the population increased to about 11 million in the metropolitan area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Edo-Tokyo_Open_Air_Architectural_Museum" title="Edo-Tokyo Open Air Architectural Museum">Edo-Tokyo Open Air Architectural Museum</a> has historic <a href="/wiki/Japanese_architecture" title="Japanese architecture">Japanese buildings</a> that existed in the urban landscape of pre-war Tokyo.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tokyo_subway" title="Tokyo subway">Tokyo's subway</a> and commuter rail network became one of the busiest in the world<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> as more and more people moved to the area. In the 1980s, <a class="external text" href="https://housingjapan.com/" rel="nofollow">real estate</a> prices skyrocketed <a href="/wiki/Japanese_asset_price_bubble" title="Japanese asset price bubble">during a real estate and debt bubble</a>. The bubble burst in the early 1990s, and many companies, banks, and individuals were caught with mortgage-backed debts while real estate was shrinking in value. A major recession followed, making the 1990s Japan's "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lost_Decade_(Japan)" title="Lost Decade (Japan)">Lost Decade</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> from which it is now slowly recovering.
</p><p>Tokyo still sees new urban developments on large lots of less profitable land. Recent projects include <a href="/wiki/Ebisu,_Shibuya" title="Ebisu, Shibuya">Ebisu</a> Garden Place, <a href="/wiki/Tenn%C5%8Dzu_Isle" title="Tennōzu Isle">Tennōzu Isle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shiodome" title="Shiodome">Shiodome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roppongi_Hills" title="Roppongi Hills">Roppongi Hills</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shinagawa,_Tokyo" title="Shinagawa, Tokyo">Shinagawa</a> (now also a <a href="/wiki/Shinkansen" title="Shinkansen">Shinkansen</a> station), and the <a href="/wiki/Marunouchi" title="Marunouchi">Marunouchi</a> side of <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Station" title="Tokyo Station">Tokyo Station</a>. Buildings of significance have been demolished for more up-to-date shopping facilities such as <a href="/wiki/Omotesando_Hills" title="Omotesando Hills">Omotesando Hills</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Land_reclamation" title="Land reclamation">Land reclamation</a> projects in Tokyo have also been going on for centuries. The most prominent is the <a href="/wiki/Odaiba" title="Odaiba">Odaiba</a> area, now a major shopping and entertainment center. Various plans have been proposed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> for transferring national government functions from Tokyo to secondary capitals in other regions of Japan, to slow down rapid development in Tokyo and revitalize economically lagging areas of the country. These plans have been controversial<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> within Japan and have yet to be realized.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 Tōhoku earthquake and tsunami">2011 Tōhoku earthquake and tsunami</a> that devastated much of the northeastern coast of Honshu was felt in Tokyo. However, due to Tokyo's earthquake-resistant infrastructure, damage in Tokyo was very minor compared to areas directly hit by the tsunami,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> although activity in the city was largely halted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The subsequent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">nuclear crisis</a> caused by the tsunami has also largely left Tokyo unaffected, despite occasional spikes in <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>On September 7, 2013, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IOC" title="IOC">IOC</a> selected Tokyo to host the <a href="/wiki/2020_Summer_Olympics" title="2020 Summer Olympics">2020 Summer Olympics</a>. Tokyo thus became the first Asian city to host the Olympic Games twice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> However, as a result of the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a>, the 2020 Olympic Games were ultimately postponed to 2021. It is also unclear how the city will deal with an increasing number of issues, urging scholars to offer possible alternatives approaches to tackle the most urgent problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Although, COVID-19 has impeded the growth of many industries, the real estate market in Japan is yet to be negatively impacted. Japanese real estate has become one of the most safest investments for foreign investors around the world. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed" style="text-align: center;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_government">Geography and government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography and government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Government" title="Tokyo Metropolitan Government">Tokyo Metropolitan Government</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_by_Sentinel-2,_2018-10-30.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5695" data-file-width="9499" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Tokyo_by_Sentinel-2%2C_2018-10-30.jpg/220px-Tokyo_by_Sentinel-2%2C_2018-10-30.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Tokyo_by_Sentinel-2%2C_2018-10-30.jpg/330px-Tokyo_by_Sentinel-2%2C_2018-10-30.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Tokyo_by_Sentinel-2%2C_2018-10-30.jpg/440px-Tokyo_by_Sentinel-2%2C_2018-10-30.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_by_Sentinel-2,_2018-10-30.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Satellite photo of Tokyo in 2018 taken by ESA Sentinel-2</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TokyoMetropolitanGovernmentOffice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2292" data-file-width="3438" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/TokyoMetropolitanGovernmentOffice.jpg/220px-TokyoMetropolitanGovernmentOffice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/TokyoMetropolitanGovernmentOffice.jpg/330px-TokyoMetropolitanGovernmentOffice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/TokyoMetropolitanGovernmentOffice.jpg/440px-TokyoMetropolitanGovernmentOffice.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TokyoMetropolitanGovernmentOffice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Government_Building" title="Tokyo Metropolitan Government Building">Tokyo Metropolitan Government Building</a></div></div></div>
<p>The mainland portion of Tokyo lies northwest of <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Bay" title="Tokyo Bay">Tokyo Bay</a> and measures about 90 km (56 mi) east to west and 25 km (16 mi) north to south. The average elevation in Tokyo is 40 m (131 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chiba_Prefecture" title="Chiba Prefecture">Chiba Prefecture</a> borders it to the east, <a href="/wiki/Yamanashi_Prefecture" title="Yamanashi Prefecture">Yamanashi</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Kanagawa_Prefecture" title="Kanagawa Prefecture">Kanagawa</a> to the south, and <a href="/wiki/Saitama_Prefecture" title="Saitama Prefecture">Saitama</a> to the north. Mainland Tokyo is further subdivided into the special wards (occupying the eastern half) and the Tama area (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">多摩地域</span></span></span>) stretching westwards. Tokyo has a <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> of 35.65 (near the <a href="/wiki/36th_parallel_north" title="36th parallel north">36th parallel north</a>), which makes it more southern than <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> (41.90), <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a> (40.41), <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> (40.71) and <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> (39.91).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also within the administrative boundaries of Tokyo Metropolis are two island chains in the Pacific Ocean directly south: the <a href="/wiki/Izu_Islands" title="Izu Islands">Izu Islands</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ogasawara_Islands" title="Ogasawara Islands">Ogasawara Islands</a>, which stretch more than 1,000 km (620 mi) away from the mainland. Because of these islands and the mountainous regions to the west, Tokyo's overall population density figures far under-represent the real figures for the urban and suburban regions of Tokyo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Japan" title="Law of Japan">Japanese law</a>, the prefecture of Tokyo is designated as a <i>to</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">都</span></span>)</span>, translated as <i><a href="/wiki/Metropolis" title="Metropolis">metropolis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Tokyo Prefecture is the most populous prefecture and the densest, with 6,100 inhabitants per square kilometre (16,000/sq mi); by geographic area it is the third-smallest, above only <a href="/wiki/Osaka_Prefecture" title="Osaka Prefecture">Osaka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kagawa_Prefecture" title="Kagawa Prefecture">Kagawa</a>. Its administrative structure is similar to that of Japan's other <a href="/wiki/Prefectures_of_Japan" title="Prefectures of Japan">prefectures</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Special_wards_of_Tokyo" title="Special wards of Tokyo">23 special wards</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">特別区</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">tokubetsu-ku</i></span>)</span>, which until 1943 constituted the <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_City" title="Tokyo City">city of Tokyo</a>, are self-governing <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Japan" title="Municipalities of Japan">municipalities</a>, each having a mayor, a council, and the status of a city.
</p><p>In addition to these 23 special wards, Tokyo also includes 26 more cities (<span lang="ja"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%B8%82#Japanese" title="wikt:市">市</a></span> <i>-shi</i>), five towns (<span lang="ja"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%94%BA#Japanese" title="wikt:町">町</a></span> <i>-chō</i> or <i>machi</i>), and eight villages (<span lang="ja"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9D%91#Japanese" title="wikt:村">村</a></span> <i>-son</i> or <i>-mura</i>), each of which has a local government. The <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Government" title="Tokyo Metropolitan Government">Tokyo Metropolitan Government</a> administers the whole metropolis including the 23 special wards and the cities and towns that constitute the prefecture. It is headed by a publicly elected governor and metropolitan assembly. Its <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Government_Building" title="Tokyo Metropolitan Government Building">headquarters</a> is in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shinjuku,_Tokyo" title="Shinjuku, Tokyo">Shinjuku</a> Ward.
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><div class="noresize"><map name="ImageMap_bb19186477d4b60a"><area alt="Okutama" coords="108,10,38,51,102,189,187,147,230,149,260,83,234,48,153,39" <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Municipalities">Municipalities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Municipalities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Tokyo_Metropolis_by_population" title="List of cities in Tokyo Metropolis by population">List of cities in Tokyo Metropolis by population</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_Nishitama_District_Area_Map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="252" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Tokyo_Nishitama_District_Area_Map.svg/220px-Tokyo_Nishitama_District_Area_Map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Tokyo_Nishitama_District_Area_Map.svg/330px-Tokyo_Nishitama_District_Area_Map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Tokyo_Nishitama_District_Area_Map.svg/440px-Tokyo_Nishitama_District_Area_Map.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_Nishitama_District_Area_Map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Nishi-Tama District in green</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Izu_Islands.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1409" data-file-width="1257" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Map_of_Izu_Islands.png/220px-Map_of_Izu_Islands.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Map_of_Izu_Islands.png/330px-Map_of_Izu_Islands.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Map_of_Izu_Islands.png/440px-Map_of_Izu_Islands.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Izu_Islands.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Izu Islands in black labels</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ogasawara_islands.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="798" data-file-width="1011" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Ogasawara_islands.png/220px-Ogasawara_islands.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Ogasawara_islands.png/330px-Ogasawara_islands.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Ogasawara_islands.png/440px-Ogasawara_islands.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ogasawara_islands.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Ogasawara Islands in black labels</div></div></div>
<p>Since 2001, Tokyo consists of 62 <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Japan" title="Municipalities of Japan">municipalities</a>: 23 <a href="/wiki/Special_wards_of_Tokyo" title="Special wards of Tokyo">special wards</a>, 26 <a href="/wiki/Cities_of_Japan" title="Cities of Japan">cities</a>, 5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Towns_of_Japan" title="Towns of Japan">towns</a> and 8 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Villages_of_Japan" title="Villages of Japan">villages</a>. Any municipality of Japan has a directly elected mayor and a directly elected assembly, each elected on independent four-year cycles. 23 of Tokyo's municipalities cover the area that had been <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_City" title="Tokyo City">Tokyo City</a> until WWII, 30 remain today in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tama_area" title="Tama area">Tama area</a> (former North Tama, West Tama and South Tama districts), 9 on Tokyo's outlying islands.
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Special_wards_of_Tokyo" title="Special wards of Tokyo">special wards</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">特別区</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">tokubetsu-ku</i></span>)</span> of Tokyo comprise the area formerly incorporated as <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_City" title="Tokyo City">Tokyo City</a>. The special wards use the word "city" in their official English name (e.g. Chiyoda City). The wards differ from other cities in having a unique administrative relationship with the prefectural government. Certain municipal functions, such as waterworks, sewerage, and fire-fighting, are handled by the Tokyo Metropolitan Government. To pay for the added administrative costs, the prefecture collects municipal taxes, which would usually be levied by the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The "three central wards" of Tokyo – Chiyoda, Chūō and Minato – are the business core of the city, with a daytime population more than seven times higher than their nighttime population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Chiyoda Ward is unique in that it is in the very heart of the former <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_City" title="Tokyo City">Tokyo City</a>, yet is one of the least populated wards. It is occupied by many major <a href="/wiki/List_of_companies_of_Japan" title="List of companies of Japan">Japanese companies</a> and is also the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Japan" title="Government of Japan">national government</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">Japanese emperor</a>. It is often called the "political center" of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Akihabara" title="Akihabara">Akihabara</a>, known for being an <a href="/wiki/Otaku" title="Otaku">otaku</a> cultural center and a shopping district for computer goods, is also in Chiyoda.</li>
<li>To the west of the special wards, Tokyo Metropolis consists of cities, towns, and villages that enjoy the same legal status as those elsewhere in Japan. While serving as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bedroom_community" title="Bedroom community">bed towns</a>" for those working in central Tokyo, some of them also have a local commercial and industrial base, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tachikawa" title="Tachikawa">Tachikawa</a>. Collectively, these are often known as the Tama area or <a href="/wiki/Western_Tokyo" title="Western Tokyo">Western Tokyo</a>. The far west of the Tama area is occupied by the district (<i>gun</i>) of <a href="/wiki/Nishitama_District,_Tokyo" title="Nishitama District, Tokyo">Nishi-Tama</a>. Much of this area is mountainous and unsuitable for urbanization. The highest mountain in Tokyo, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kumotori" title="Mount Kumotori">Mount Kumotori</a>, is 2,017 m (6,617 ft) high; other mountains in Tokyo include Takanosu (1,737 m (5,699 ft)), Odake (1,266 m (4,154 ft)), and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Mitake_(Tokyo)" title="Mount Mitake (Tokyo)">Mitake</a> (929 m (3,048 ft)). <a href="/wiki/Lake_Okutama" title="Lake Okutama">Lake Okutama</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/Tama_River" title="Tama River">Tama River</a> near <a href="/wiki/Yamanashi_Prefecture" title="Yamanashi Prefecture">Yamanashi Prefecture</a>, is Tokyo's largest lake. The district is composed of three towns (<a href="/wiki/Hinode,_Tokyo" title="Hinode, Tokyo">Hinode</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mizuho,_Tokyo" title="Mizuho, Tokyo">Mizuho</a> and <a href="/wiki/Okutama,_Tokyo" title="Okutama, Tokyo">Okutama</a>) and one village (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hinohara,_Tokyo" title="Hinohara, Tokyo">Hinohara</a>). The Tokyo Metropolitan Government has designated Hachiōji, Tachikawa, Machida, Ōme and Tama New Town as regional centers of the Tama area,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> as part of its plans to relocate urban functions away from central Tokyo.</li>
<li>Tokyo has numerous outlying islands, which extend as far as 1,850 km (1,150 mi) from central Tokyo. Because of the islands' distance from the administrative headquarters of the Tokyo Metropolitan Government in Shinjuku, local subprefectural branch offices administer them. The <a href="/wiki/Izu_Islands" title="Izu Islands">Izu Islands</a> are a group of volcanic islands and form part of the <a href="/wiki/Fuji-Hakone-Izu_National_Park" title="Fuji-Hakone-Izu National Park">Fuji-Hakone-Izu National Park</a>. The islands in order from closest to Tokyo are <a href="/wiki/Izu_%C5%8Cshima" title="Izu Ōshima">Izu Ōshima</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toshima_Island" title="Toshima Island">Toshima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nii-jima" title="Nii-jima">Nii-jima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shikine-jima" title="Shikine-jima">Shikine-jima</a>, <a href="/wiki/K%C5%8Dzu-shima" title="Kōzu-shima">Kōzu-shima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miyake-jima" title="Miyake-jima">Miyake-jima</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mikurajima" title="Mikurajima">Mikurajima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hachij%C5%8D-jima" title="Hachijō-jima">Hachijō-jima</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aogashima" title="Aogashima">Aogashima</a>. The Izu Islands are grouped into three subprefectures. Izu Ōshima and Hachijojima are towns. The remaining islands are six villages, with Niijima and Shikinejima forming <a href="/wiki/Niijima,_Tokyo" title="Niijima, Tokyo">one village</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bonin_Islands" title="Bonin Islands">Ogasawara Islands</a> include, from north to south, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chichi-jima" title="Chichi-jima">Chichi-jima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nishinoshima_(Ogasawara)" title="Nishinoshima (Ogasawara)">Nishinoshima</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haha-jima" title="Haha-jima">Haha-jima</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Iwo_Jima" title="North Iwo Jima">Kita Iwo Jima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iwo_Jima" title="Iwo Jima">Iwo Jima</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minami_Iwo_Jima" title="Minami Iwo Jima">Minami Iwo Jima</a>. Ogasawara also administers two tiny outlying islands: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minami_Torishima" title="Minami Torishima">Minami Torishima</a>, the easternmost point in Japan and at 1,850 km (1,150 mi) the most distant island from central Tokyo, and <a href="/wiki/Okinotorishima" title="Okinotorishima">Okinotorishima</a>, the southernmost point in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Japan's claim on an <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">exclusive economic zone</a> (EEZ) surrounding Okinotorishima is contested by <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> as they regard Okinotorishima as uninhabitable rocks which have no EEZ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The Iwo chain and the outlying islands have no permanent population, but hosts <a href="/wiki/Japan_Self-Defense_Forces" title="Japan Self-Defense Forces">Japan Self-Defense Forces</a> personnel. Local populations are only found on Chichi-Jima and Haha-Jima. The islands form both <a href="/wiki/Ogasawara_Subprefecture" title="Ogasawara Subprefecture">Ogasawara Subprefecture</a> and the village of <a href="/wiki/Ogasawara,_Tokyo" title="Ogasawara, Tokyo">Ogasawara, Tokyo</a>.</li></ul>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Municipal_mergers">Municipal mergers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Municipal mergers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_mergers_in_Tokyo" title="List of mergers in Tokyo">List of mergers in Tokyo</a></div>
<p>When Tokyo reached its current extent except for smaller border changes in 1893, it consisted of over 170 municipalities, 1 (by definition: district-independent) city, nine <a href="/wiki/Districts_of_Japan" title="Districts of Japan">districts</a> with their towns and villages, plus the island communities that had never part of ritsuryō<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> districts. By 1953, the number of municipalities had dropped to 97. The current total of 62 was reached in 2001.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_parks">National parks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: National parks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ogasawara_Islands,_Tokyo,_Japan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1252" data-file-width="1882" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ogasawara_Islands%2C_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg/220px-Ogasawara_Islands%2C_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ogasawara_Islands%2C_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg/330px-Ogasawara_Islands%2C_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ogasawara_Islands%2C_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg/440px-Ogasawara_Islands%2C_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ogasawara_Islands,_Tokyo,_Japan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ogasawara National Park, a UNESCO World Natural Heritage Site</div></div></div>
<p>As of March 31, 2008, 36% of the total land area of the prefecture was designated as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prefectural_Natural_Park" title="Prefectural Natural Park">Natural Parks</a> (second only to <a href="/wiki/Shiga_Prefecture" title="Shiga Prefecture">Shiga Prefecture</a>), namely the <a href="/wiki/Chichibu_Tama_Kai_National_Park" title="Chichibu Tama Kai National Park">Chichibu Tama Kai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fuji-Hakone-Izu_National_Park" title="Fuji-Hakone-Izu National Park">Fuji-Hakone-Izu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ogasawara_National_Park" title="Ogasawara National Park">Ogasawara</a> National Parks (the last a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Sites_in_Japan" title="World Heritage Sites in Japan">World Heritage Site</a>); <a href="/wiki/Meiji_no_Mori_Takao_Quasi-National_Park" title="Meiji no Mori Takao Quasi-National Park">Meiji no Mori Takao Quasi-National Park</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Akikawa_Ky%C5%ABry%C5%8D_Prefectural_Natural_Park" title="Akikawa Kyūryō Prefectural Natural Park">Akikawa Kyūryō</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamura_Kusabana_Ky%C5%ABry%C5%8D_Prefectural_Natural_Park" title="Hamura Kusabana Kyūryō Prefectural Natural Park">Hamura Kusabana Kyūryō</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sayama_Prefectural_Natural_Park_(Tokyo)" title="Sayama Prefectural Natural Park (Tokyo)">Sayama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Takao_Jinba_Prefectural_Natural_Park" title="Takao Jinba Prefectural Natural Park">Takao Jinba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Takiyama_Prefectural_Natural_Park" title="Takiyama Prefectural Natural Park">Takiyama</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tama_Ky%C5%ABry%C5%8D_Prefectural_Natural_Park" title="Tama Kyūryō Prefectural Natural Park">Tama Kyūryō</a> Prefectural Natural Parks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of museums are located in <a href="/wiki/Ueno_Park" title="Ueno Park">Ueno Park</a>: <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_National_Museum" title="Tokyo National Museum">Tokyo National Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Nature_and_Science" title="National Museum of Nature and Science">National Museum of Nature and Science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shitamachi_Museum" title="Shitamachi Museum">Shitamachi Museum</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Western_Art" title="National Museum of Western Art">National Museum for Western Art</a>, among others. There are also artworks and statues at several places in the park. There is also a zoo in the park, and the park is a popular destination to view cherry blossoms.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earthquakes">Earthquakes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Earthquakes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_quakes">Minor quakes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Minor quakes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Temporarygatheringplace.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="2848" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Temporarygatheringplace.jpg/220px-Temporarygatheringplace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Temporarygatheringplace.jpg/330px-Temporarygatheringplace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Temporarygatheringplace.jpg/440px-Temporarygatheringplace.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Temporarygatheringplace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bilingual sign with instructions (in Japanese and English) in case of an earthquake (Shibuya)</div></div></div>
<p>Tokyo is near the <a href="/wiki/Boso_Triple_Junction" title="Boso Triple Junction">boundary of three plates</a>, making it an extremely active region for smaller quakes and <a href="/wiki/Slow_earthquake" title="Slow earthquake">slippage</a> which frequently affect the urban area with swaying as if in a boat, although epicenters within mainland Tokyo (excluding Tokyo's 2,000 km (1,243 mi)–long island jurisdiction) are quite rare. It is not uncommon in the metro area to have hundreds of these minor quakes (magnitudes 4–6) that can be felt in a single year, something local residents merely brush off but can be a source of anxiety not only for foreign visitors but for Japanese from elsewhere as well. They rarely cause much damage (sometimes a few injuries) as they are either too small or far away as quakes tend to dance around the region. Particularly active are offshore regions and to a lesser extent <a href="/wiki/Chiba_Prefecture" title="Chiba Prefecture">Chiba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibaraki_Prefecture" title="Ibaraki Prefecture">Ibaraki</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrequent_powerful_quakes">Infrequent powerful quakes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Infrequent powerful quakes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Tokyo has been hit by powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megathrust" title="Megathrust">megathrust</a> earthquakes in 1703, 1782, 1812, 1855, 1923, and much more indirectly (with some <a href="/wiki/Soil_liquefaction" title="Soil liquefaction">liquefaction</a> in landfill zones) in <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 Tōhoku earthquake and tsunami">2011</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> the frequency of direct and large quakes is a relative rarity. The 1923 earthquake, with an estimated magnitude of 8.3, killed 142,000 people, the last time the urban area was directly hit.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Volcanic_eruptions">Volcanic eruptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Volcanic eruptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mount_Fuji" title="Mount Fuji">Mount Fuji</a> is about 100 km (62 mi) southwest of Tokyo. There is a low risk of eruption. The last recorded was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H%C5%8Dei_eruption_of_Mount_Fuji" title="Hōei eruption of Mount Fuji">Hōei eruption</a> which started on December 16, 1707, and ended about January 1, 1708 (16 days).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShizuokaUni_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShizuokaUni-63">[63]</a></sup> During the Hōei eruption, the ash amount was 4 cm in southern Tokyo (bay area) and 2 cm to 0.5 cm in central Tokyo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoei-eruption-map_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoei-eruption-map-64">[64]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanagawa" title="Kanagawa">Kanagawa</a> had 16 cm to 8 cm ash and <a href="/wiki/Saitama_Prefecture" title="Saitama Prefecture">Saitama</a> 0.5 to 0 cm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoei-eruption-map_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoei-eruption-map-64">[64]</a></sup> If the wind blows north-east it could send <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_ash" title="Volcanic ash">volcanic ash</a> to Tokyo metropolis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuji-eruption_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-fuji-eruption-65">[65]</a></sup> According to the government, less than a millimeter of the volcanic ash from a Mount Fuji eruption could cause power grid problems such as blackouts and stop trains in the Tokyo metropolitan area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuji-eruption_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-fuji-eruption-65">[65]</a></sup> A mixture of ash with rain could stick to cellphone antennas, power lines and cause temporary power outages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuji-eruption_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-fuji-eruption-65">[65]</a></sup> The affected areas would need to be evacuated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuji-eruption_65-3"><a href="#cite_note-fuji-eruption-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_management">Water management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Water management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geofront_Temple%5E_%E9%A6%96%E9%83%BD%E5%9C%8F%E5%A4%96%E9%83%AD%E6%94%BE%E6%B0%B4%E8%B7%AF_-_panoramio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Geofront_Temple%5E_%E9%A6%96%E9%83%BD%E5%9C%8F%E5%A4%96%E9%83%AD%E6%94%BE%E6%B0%B4%E8%B7%AF_-_panoramio.jpg/220px-Geofront_Temple%5E_%E9%A6%96%E9%83%BD%E5%9C%8F%E5%A4%96%E9%83%AD%E6%94%BE%E6%B0%B4%E8%B7%AF_-_panoramio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Geofront_Temple%5E_%E9%A6%96%E9%83%BD%E5%9C%8F%E5%A4%96%E9%83%AD%E6%94%BE%E6%B0%B4%E8%B7%AF_-_panoramio.jpg/330px-Geofront_Temple%5E_%E9%A6%96%E9%83%BD%E5%9C%8F%E5%A4%96%E9%83%AD%E6%94%BE%E6%B0%B4%E8%B7%AF_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Geofront_Temple%5E_%E9%A6%96%E9%83%BD%E5%9C%8F%E5%A4%96%E9%83%AD%E6%94%BE%E6%B0%B4%E8%B7%AF_-_panoramio.jpg/440px-Geofront_Temple%5E_%E9%A6%96%E9%83%BD%E5%9C%8F%E5%A4%96%E9%83%AD%E6%94%BE%E6%B0%B4%E8%B7%AF_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geofront_Temple%5E_%E9%A6%96%E9%83%BD%E5%9C%8F%E5%A4%96%E9%83%AD%E6%94%BE%E6%B0%B4%E8%B7%AF_-_panoramio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Area_Outer_Underground_Discharge_Channel" title="Metropolitan Area Outer Underground Discharge Channel">MAOUDC</a> is the world's largest underground diversion floodwater facility.</div></div></div>
<p>Tokyo is located on the <a href="/wiki/Kant%C5%8D_Plain" title="Kantō Plain">Kantō Plain</a> with 5 river systems and dozens of rivers that expand during each season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood-protection_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-flood-protection-66">[66]</a></sup> Important rivers are <a href="/wiki/Edogawa,_Tokyo" title="Edogawa, Tokyo">Edogawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naka_River_(Saitama_Tokyo)" title="Naka River (Saitama Tokyo)">Nakagawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arakawa_River_(Kant%C5%8D)" title="Arakawa River (Kantō)">Arakawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kanda_River" title="Kanda River">Kandagawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meguro_River" title="Meguro River">Megurogawa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tama_River" title="Tama River">Tamagawa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood-tokyo_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-flood-tokyo-67">[67]</a></sup> In 1947 <a href="/wiki/Typhoon_Kathleen" title="Typhoon Kathleen">Typhoon Kathleen</a> struck Tokyo, destroying 31,000 homes and killing 1,100 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood-protection_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-flood-protection-66">[66]</a></sup> In 1958 <a href="/wiki/Typhoon_Ida_(1958)" title="Typhoon Ida (1958)">Typhoon Ida</a> inflicted 400 mm rain in 1 week which flooded streets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood-protection_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-flood-protection-66">[66]</a></sup> In the 1950s and 1960s, the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Japan" title="Government of Japan">government</a> invested 6–7% of the national budget on disaster and risk reduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood-protection_66-3"><a href="#cite_note-flood-protection-66">[66]</a></sup> A huge system of dams, levees and tunnels was constructed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood-protection_66-4"><a href="#cite_note-flood-protection-66">[66]</a></sup> The purpose is to manage heavy rain, <a href="/wiki/Typhoon" title="Typhoon">typhonic</a> rain, and river floods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood-protection_66-5"><a href="#cite_note-flood-protection-66">[66]</a></sup> Tokyo has currently the world's largest underground floodwater diversion facility called the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Area_Outer_Underground_Discharge_Channel" title="Metropolitan Area Outer Underground Discharge Channel">Metropolitan Area Outer Underground Discharge Channel</a> (MAOUDC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maoudc_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-maoudc-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood-protection_66-6"><a href="#cite_note-flood-protection-66">[66]</a></sup> It took 13 years to build and was completed in 2006. The MAOUDC is a 6.3 km long system of tunnels, 22 meters underground, with 70 meter tall cylindrical tanks, where each tank is large enough to fit a space shuttle or the Statue of Liberty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood-protection_66-7"><a href="#cite_note-flood-protection-66">[66]</a></sup> During floods, excess water is collected from rivers and drained to the <a href="/wiki/Edo_River" title="Edo River">Edo River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood-tokyo_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-flood-tokyo-67">[67]</a></sup> Low-lying areas of <a href="/wiki/K%C5%8Dt%C5%8D" title="Kōtō">Kōtō</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edogawa,_Tokyo" title="Edogawa, Tokyo">Edogawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sumida,_Tokyo" title="Sumida, Tokyo">Sumida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Katsushika" title="Katsushika">Katsushika</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tait%C5%8D" title="Taitō">Taitō</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arakawa,_Tokyo" title="Arakawa, Tokyo">Arakawa</a> near the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arakawa_River_(Kanto)" title="Arakawa River (Kanto)">Arakawa River</a> are most at risk of flooding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood-tokyo_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-flood-tokyo-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The former city of Tokyo and the majority of Tokyo prefecture lie in the <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">humid subtropical climate</a> zone (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> <i>Cfa</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> with hot, humid summers and mild to cool winters with occasional cold spells. The region, like much of Japan, experiences a one-month <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_lag" title="Seasonal lag">seasonal lag</a>, with the warmest month being August, which averages 26.4 °C (79.5 °F), and the coolest month being January, averaging 5.2 °C (41.4 °F). The record low temperature is −9.2 °C (15.4 °F) on January 13, 1876, while the record high is 39.5 °C (103.1 °F) on July 20, 2004. 
The record highest low temperature is 30.3 °C (86.5 °F) on August 12, 2013, making Tokyo one of only seven observation sites in Japan that have recorded a low temperature over 30 °C (86.0 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JMArecords_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-JMArecords-69">[69]</a></sup> Annual rainfall averages nearly 1,530 millimeters (60.2 in), with a wetter summer and a drier winter. The growing season in Tokyo lasts for about 322 days from around mid February to early January.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Snowfall is sporadic, but does occur almost annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Tokyo also often sees typhoons every year, though few are strong. The wettest month since records began in 1876 was October 2004, with 780 millimeters (30 in) of rain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> including 270.5 mm (10.65 in) on the ninth of that month;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> the last of four months on record to observe no precipitation is December 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JMArecords_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-JMArecords-69">[69]</a></sup> Annual precipitation has ranged from 879.5 mm (34.63 in) in 1984 to 2,229.6 mm (87.78 in) in 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JMArecords_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-JMArecords-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>
<p>The climates of Tokyo's offshore territories vary significantly from those of the city. The climate of <a href="/wiki/Chichijima" title="Chichijima">Chichijima</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ogasawara,_Tokyo" title="Ogasawara, Tokyo">Ogasawara village</a> is on the boundary between the <a href="/wiki/Tropical_savanna_climate" title="Tropical savanna climate">tropical savanna climate</a> (Köppen classification <i>Aw</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest_climate" title="Tropical rainforest climate">tropical rainforest climate</a> (Köppen classification <i>Af</i>). It is approximately 1,000 km (621 mi) south of the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Tokyo_Area" title="Greater Tokyo Area">Greater Tokyo Area</a> resulting in much different climatic conditions.
</p>
